Interpreting Capacity and Refuting a False View
Maturidi interprets capacity verse and refutes a false view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's interpretation of the verse on capacity and his refutation of a view that would make God's obligation exceed human capacity.

Core Idea
God never obligates beyond what a person can bear; justice requires alignment of obligation and capacity.

Maturidi begins by reaffirming the correct interpretation of a key Quranic verse. He then explicitly rejects a view that would nullify obligation at the time of the act, which would effectively burden a soul beyond its capacity. This sets the foundation for a deeper discussion on divine justice and human responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Start by reading the original Arabic and its literal translation to grasp Maturidi's concise style.
Notice how he uses 'qabih' (ugly) to describe the refuted view.
2
Identify the two parts of the passage
The passage has two main components: the interpretation of the verse and the refutation of a false opinion. Locate each.
The refutation is indicated by 'wa bayyana qubha qawlihi'.
3
Understand why the refuted view is ugly
Maturidi argues that dropping obligation at the time of act and negating power makes the obligated person beyond capacity, which contradicts divine justice.
This connects to the broader principle that God does not burden a soul beyond its capacity.
4
Reflect on the relevance
Consider how this refutation upholds the balance between divine decree and human accountability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد بينا تأويل قوله: لا يُكَلِّفُ اللهُ نَفْسًا إِلَّا وُسْعَهَا، وبينا قبح قوله في إسقاط التكليف وقت الفعل وإبطال القدرة عليه، فيصير في التحصيل المكلف على غير الوسع. على أنه يقال : كيف لو كان في علم لا يفعل، أو في علمه أنه يريد الفعل في الوقت الذي يتلوه، ومن قولكم إن من أراد الفعل في الوقت الذي يتلوه إنه يفعله لا محالة، إلا أن يمنع أن يفعله، أيمنع أو يفعل ضده؟ فإن قال : يفعل ضده أبطل قوله في كون الإرادة قبل الفعل وألزم نفسه الأمر معه والقدرة معه وبطل قولهم في الإرادة الموجبة، وإن قال : يمنع، فقد ألزم من فى علم الله أنه لا يفعل التكليف بقوة تمنع عن الفعل، وهو في التحقيق تكليف العاجز الممنوع، وإن كان برفع الكلفة أبطل أن يكون أحد ممن في علم الله أنه لا يعطيه مما تضمنته المحنة ولزمه الأمر والنهي، وذلك غاية ما ينتهى إليه القول في القبح. وعلى ذلك أمر الإرادة، إن الله إذ يمنعه عن الفعل الذي في حكمه أنه لا يفعله لا بد أن يمنعه كالإرادة، وفى ذلك منع عن الطاعة إذ يمنعه عن الفعل الذى فى علمه أنه لا يفعله لا بد أن يمنعه بالإرادة، وفى ذلك منع عن الطاعة عنده والخير. ثم يقال : المروى عن الذي روى أنه سنل سيفه على رسول الله فمنعه الله بقبض يده، أكان ذلك المنع أصلح له في الدين وأخير له أو الإطلاق ؟ فإن قال : الإطلاق، فقد أقرّ بأن الله قد يفعل بعباده.
And we have explained the interpretation of His saying: 'Allah does not charge a soul except [within] its capacity' (2:286). And we have explained the ugliness of his saying in dropping obligation at the time of act and nullifying power upon it, so that in actuality the obligated is beyond capacity. Moreover, it is said: How if it is in [His] knowledge that he will not act, or in His knowledge that he intends the act at the time that follows, and your saying that whoever intends the act at the time that follows, he will do it necessarily, unless prevented from doing it? Does He prevent or does He make him do its opposite? If he says: He makes him do its opposite, he nullifies his saying that will is before the act, and imposes upon himself that command and ability are with it, and their saying about the necessitating will is invalid. And if he says: He prevents, then he imposes upon one whom God knows will not act, the obligation with a power that prevents from the act, and that is in reality an obligation upon the incapable prevented one. And if it is by raising the burden, he nullifies that anyone about whom God knows that He will not give him from what the trial entails, and the command and prohibition become necessary for him. And that is the utmost extent of the saying about ugliness. And upon that is the matter of will. Indeed, when God prevents him from the act which in His judgment he will not do, He must prevent him, such as by will. And in that is prevention from obedience, as He prevents him from the act which in His knowledge he will not do, He must prevent him by will. And in that is prevention from obedience and good in his view. Then it is said: The narrated [story] about the one who drew his sword upon the Messenger of Allah, and Allah prevented him by grasping his hand: Was that prevention better for him in religion and more beneficial for him, or the letting go? If he says: The letting go, then he has affirmed that Allah may do to His servants...
Source pages p. 868

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument about divine justice and human capacity. Maturidi interprets the Quranic verse (2:286) that God does not burden a soul beyond its capacity. He refutes a view that nullifies obligation (taklif) at the time of action and invalidates power (qudra) upon it, which would effectively obligate beyond capacity. Then he engages in a dialectical analysis: If God knows a person will not perform an act, or knows he intends it at a future time, then according to the opponent's own principle, the person will necessarily do it unless prevented. Maturidi examines two possibilities: God either makes him do the opposite or prevents him. Both lead to contradictions. The first nullifies the opponent's claim that will precedes the act; the second imposes obligation on one who is incapable (because prevented). This shows that God's knowledge and will do not conflict with justice. Then he uses the story of a man who drew his sword on the Prophet and was prevented by God. The question: was that prevention better for him in religion or releasing him? This tests the opponent's view about God's actions being for the best (al-aslah). The implication is that God's actions are wise even if they appear to prevent good.

Key Terms
التكليف
الوسع
القدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God might obligate a person to do something they are incapable of.
Correction: Maturidi shows that such a view is 'qabih' (ugly) because the verse explicitly states that God's obligation is within capacity; any opinion suggesting otherwise is a misinterpretation.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what happens if obligation is dropped at the time of the act and power is nullified?
A) The person becomes excused.
B) The person is obligated beyond capacity.
C) The person is still obligated.
D) The person has free will.

Key Takeaway
Divine obligation always aligns with human capacity; any view that suggests otherwise is both incorrect and morally objectionable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a difficult religious obligation, do you ever doubt that you have the capacity to fulfill it? How does this verse challenge that doubt?
?
Reflect on a time you felt that an obligation was too heavy for you. In light of this lesson, what might you have been misunderstanding about God's justice and your own capacity?
?
Are there areas in your life where you have dropped an obligation, thinking it was beyond you, without considering that the power to act comes at the moment of action? What will you do about it starting today?

Dialectical Analysis of Knowledge, Will, and Prevention
Maturidi presents a dialectical argument on knowledge and will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's dialectical argument showing that God's knowledge and will do not conflict with justice, and how two possibilities both lead to contradictions for the opponent.

Core Idea
God's foreknowledge and will do not impose injustice; the opponent's own principles lead to absurdities.

Maturidi now engages in a sophisticated dialectical exchange with the opponent. He examines two scenarios based on God's knowledge and the opponent's claim about the necessity of action when will exists. By exploring the outcomes of prevention versus causing the opposite, he demonstrates that the opponent's position collapses into self-contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read carefully to follow the conditional reasoning and the two branches of the argument.
Pay attention to the phrase 'aiman'a aw yaf'alu diddahu?' (Does He prevent or make him do its opposite?).
2
Trace the first possibility: causing the opposite
If God makes the person do the opposite of the intended act, the opponent's claim that will precedes the act is nullified. Also, it implies that command and ability coincide with the act, invalidating the idea of a necessitating will.
3
Trace the second possibility: prevention
If God prevents the act, then the obligation is placed on someone who is incapable due to prevention, which is an obligation on the incapable and thus unjust.
Maturidi calls this 'taklif al-'ajiz al-mamnu'' (obligation of the prevented incapable).
4
Understand the final conclusion
Both branches lead to absurdities, showing the opponent's position on God's will and human action is untenable.
Maturidi concludes this section by stating that this is the utmost extent of the saying about ugliness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the consequence of saying God causes the opposite of the intended act?
A) It affirms divine mercy.
B) It nullifies the opponent's claim that will precedes the act.

D) It justifies God's prevention.

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever find yourself blaming God for your failures, thinking 'He knew I would sin, so it was out of my control'? How does Maturidi's argument challenge that excuse? Will you stop using God's foreknowledge as a shield and instead take full responsibility for your choices starting today?
?
The opponent in this passage assumed that if God prevents an act, it would be unjust. But what about your own heart? Are there areas where you feel God has 'prevented' you from doing good, leaving you feeling unjustly treated? How will you respond to that feeling with the truth that God's justice is perfect and that He never obligates you beyond your capacity?
?
If both 'God causes the opposite' and 'God prevents' lead to absurdity, then God's knowledge and will are perfectly compatible with your free choices. How will this truth change the way you approach your next difficult decision? Will you act with confidence that your efforts are meaningful and that God's will does not crush your responsibility?

Prevention, Will, and the Story of the Sword
Maturidi discusses prevention by will and uses a story to challenge al-aslah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that God's prevention by will is coherent, and his use of a historical example to challenge the doctrine of al-aslah (the best).

Core Idea
God's prevention by will is part of divine wisdom; even prevention can be better than letting an act occur.

Maturidi continues by connecting prevention to divine will. He argues that when God prevents an act He knows will not occur, the prevention must be by His will. This prevention, though it appears to hinder obedience, is part of divine justice. He then invokes a concrete historical example: a man who drew his sword on the Prophet Muhammad and was prevented by God. This story serves to test the opponent's doctrine that God always does what is most beneficial (al-aslah).

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the whole passage to see how Maturidi links prevention to will and to the story.
Notice the shift from abstract argument to concrete example.
2
Understand the role of will in prevention
Maturidi asserts that God's prevention is necessarily by will, and this prevention includes preventing obedience and good from that person's perspective.
This counters the idea that God's will always leads to good for the person.
3
Analyze the story of the sword
The story asks: was the prevention (grasping the hand) better for that man in religion or the letting go (allowing him to strike) better? If the opponent says letting go is better, then he admits God may not do the most beneficial for His servants, undermining his own doctrine.
4
Draw the implication
Maturidi uses this to show that God's actions are not bound by what humans consider 'best'; divine wisdom may involve prevention that appears contrary to benefit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد بينا تأويل قوله: لا يُكَلِّفُ اللهُ نَفْسًا إِلَّا وُسْعَهَا، وبينا قبح قوله في إسقاط التكليف وقت الفعل وإبطال القدرة عليه، فيصير في التحصيل المكلف على غير الوسع. على أنه يقال : كيف لو كان في علم لا يفعل، أو في علمه أنه يريد الفعل في الوقت الذي يتلوه، ومن قولكم إن من أراد الفعل في الوقت الذي يتلوه إنه يفعله لا محالة، إلا أن يمنع أن يفعله، أيمنع أو يفعل ضده؟ فإن قال : يفعل ضده أبطل قوله في كون الإرادة قبل الفعل وألزم نفسه الأمر معه والقدرة معه وبطل قولهم في الإرادة الموجبة، وإن قال : يمنع، فقد ألزم من فى علم الله أنه لا يفعل التكليف بقوة تمنع عن الفعل، وهو في التحقيق تكليف العاجز الممنوع، وإن كان برفع الكلفة أبطل أن يكون أحد ممن في علم الله أنه لا يعطيه مما تضمنته المحنة ولزمه الأمر والنهي، وذلك غاية ما ينتهى إليه القول في القبح. وعلى ذلك أمر الإرادة، إن الله إذ يمنعه عن الفعل الذي في حكمه أنه لا يفعله لا بد أن يمنعه كالإرادة، وفى ذلك منع عن الطاعة إذ يمنعه عن الفعل الذى فى علمه أنه لا يفعله لا بد أن يمنعه بالإرادة، وفى ذلك منع عن الطاعة عنده والخير. ثم يقال : المروى عن الذي روى أنه سنل سيفه على رسول الله فمنعه الله بقبض يده، أكان ذلك المنع أصلح له في الدين وأخير له أو الإطلاق ؟ فإن قال : الإطلاق، فقد أقرّ بأن الله قد يفعل بعباده.
And we have explained the interpretation of His saying: 'Allah does not charge a soul except [within] its capacity' (2:286). And we have explained the ugliness of his saying in dropping obligation at the time of act and nullifying power upon it, so that in actuality the obligated is beyond capacity. Moreover, it is said: How if it is in [His] knowledge that he will not act, or in His knowledge that he intends the act at the time that follows, and your saying that whoever intends the act at the time that follows, he will do it necessarily, unless prevented from doing it? Does He prevent or does He make him do its opposite? If he says: He makes him do its opposite, he nullifies his saying that will is before the act, and imposes upon himself that command and ability are with it, and their saying about the necessitating will is invalid. And if he says: He prevents, then he imposes upon one whom God knows will not act, the obligation with a power that prevents from the act, and that is in reality an obligation upon the incapable prevented one. And if it is by raising the burden, he nullifies that anyone about whom God knows that He will not give him from what the trial entails, and the command and prohibition become necessary for him. And that is the utmost extent of the saying about ugliness. And upon that is the matter of will. Indeed, when God prevents him from the act which in His judgment he will not do, He must prevent him, such as by will. And in that is prevention from obedience, as He prevents him from the act which in His knowledge he will not do, He must prevent him by will. And in that is prevention from obedience and good in his view. Then it is said: The narrated [story] about the one who drew his sword upon the Messenger of Allah, and Allah prevented him by grasping his hand: Was that prevention better for him in religion and more beneficial for him, or the letting go? If he says: The letting go, then he has affirmed that Allah may do to His servants...
Source pages p. 868

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument about divine justice and human capacity. Maturidi interprets the Quranic verse (2:286) that God does not burden a soul beyond its capacity. He refutes a view that nullifies obligation (taklif) at the time of action and invalidates power (qudra) upon it, which would effectively obligate beyond capacity. Then he engages in a dialectical analysis: If God knows a person will not perform an act, or knows he intends it at a future time, then according to the opponent's own principle, the person will necessarily do it unless prevented. Maturidi examines two possibilities: God either makes him do the opposite or prevents him. Both lead to contradictions. The first nullifies the opponent's claim that will precedes the act; the second imposes obligation on one who is incapable (because prevented). This shows that God's knowledge and will do not conflict with justice. Then he uses the story of a man who drew his sword on the Prophet and was prevented by God. The question: was that prevention better for him in religion or releasing him? This tests the opponent's view about God's actions being for the best (al-aslah). The implication is that God's actions are wise even if they appear to prevent good.

Key Terms
المنع
الإرادة
الأصلح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God always does what is most beneficial (al-aslah) for each individual in every situation.
Correction: Maturidi uses the story to argue that God may prevent an act that seems beneficial from a human perspective, yet this prevention is part of divine wisdom and justice.

Checkpoint
In the story of the man who drew his sword on the Prophet, what does Maturidi argue would follow if the opponent says letting go would have been better?
A) God always does what is best.
B) God may not do what is most beneficial for His servants.
C) The man was not responsible.
D) Prevention was unjust.

Key Takeaway
Divine prevention by will is rational, and it challenges the claim that God always does the most beneficial for each person, affirming His absolute wisdom.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The man who drew his sword on the Prophet thought he was doing right, but God prevented him. What are you holding on to that you think is good, but God might be preventing you from? Are you willing to see His prevention as better than your plan?
?
If you were in that man's place, would you have thanked God for stopping you, or would you have resented being prevented? What does your honest reaction reveal about your trust in divine wisdom over your own desires?
?
This lesson shows that God's actions are not always aligned with our limited sense of 'best.' Where in your life do you struggle to accept that God's prevention or withholding might be more wise and beneficial than what you want? Will you submit to His will today?

The Third Argument Against Dualism
Maturidi presents the third argument against dualism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's third argument against dualism, which relies on the mutual ignorance of two supposed deities.

Core Idea
Two gods would each be ignorant of the other's actions, contradicting divinity.

Maturidi presents a logical argument against dualism: if there were two gods, each would have power over what the other does not know, implying ignorance. This would corrupt the very concept of divinity. Let's examine the passage and see why this argument is decisive.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Notice how Maturidi states his argument concisely.
The passage is short but dense.
2
Identify the core claim
The key claim is that the only way to recognize the falsity of dualism is through the mutual ignorance of the two supposed gods.
3
Understand the consequence
If mutual ignorance were acceptable, then monotheist arguments would be null. This shows the argument's foundational role.
Maturidi uses this to justify his refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثالث أن طريق معرفة فساد القول باثنين ليس إلا قدرة أحدها على ما لا يعلمه الآخر ، وفى ذلك إيجاب ذلك ، ولو جاز ذا من غير أن يكون في ذلك فساد الألوهية لبطل قول الموحدين فيها به أبطلوا قول الثنوية . وقوله : أنه يعلم مع لو أنه لا آمن كيف يكون ، فهذا معنى لا منفعة فيه ؛ لأنه علمه بذلك يعلم يؤمن أولا ، فإن قال : لا ، سفهه ، وإن قال : نعم ، قيل : في ذلك وقعت المطالبة ، وقد ذكرت أنه لو آمن لم يخرج من علمه ، فكيف لم يخرج ؟ وعلمه ، وقد كان ، ولا قوة إلا بالله وأما قوله : لو لم يقدر عليه لم يكن ملوما ، فهو مثل القول سواء ؛ ودليله أنه قوله ، بل عليه أعظم اللائمة ؛ هو صنع القدرة حيث أعرض عن الذى به يأتيه . وقوله لا يلزمنا لأن القدرة غير الايمان ، يدفع أيضا فما فيه ما يمنع اللزوم ، بل إنما لزم ذلك ؛ لأن القدرة غير الإيمان. ثم اعتباره بالأمر فاسد ؛ لأنه استعباد به ، يظهر ذله وعبوديته ، والقوة هى الغنى والعلو والرفعة ، فهو الوجه الذي به يبطل ربوبيته غيرا لله ، وليس فى الأمر ذلك ، على أنه لو لم يكن أمر ولا نهى كان القول. بيؤمن ويكفر ويقدر ولا يقدر لا معنى له ، ولا قوة إلا بالله و بعد ، فإن القدرة ثمرتها الفعل ، وبه يكون الذى ذكر ، لا بالأمر ؛ لذلك يصر الأمر بالأمر أمرا بالذي ذكرت ، فبالإقدار يصير ملكا غنيا مستخلفا لما إذا تم كان ربا إلها كان ربا إلها ، ولا قوة إلا بالله . وبعد ، فإنا عارضنا بالذى طريق العلم به العقل من الوجه الذي ذكرت ، والأمر لا يناقض ما يوجبه العقل ، ولولا الأمر كان الأول بالعقل وحشيا ، فأما أن يعرف وجه الحكمة في الأمر أو لا يعرفه بالذي عرفناه لم يجب دفعه بما يتعذر عليه وجه الثانى ، ولا قوة إلا بالله وأيد الذي ذكرت أمر الشاهد أن كل قوى يرتفع ويجل بقوته ولا يؤمر الجليل العظيم بشيء ، ثبت أن فى الأمر ذلة واستعبادا ، فهو لا يوجب وفى الإقدار رفعه وعلوه فهو يوجب ، والله المرفق تری ذلك ، لم أنه قد نقض 10 وبعد ، فإذ لا توجد قدرة لا تضيع فوجودها يوجب الفعل الذي يقصد ، وحق الأمر اللزوم لا وجرد الفعل ، وكم من أمر به لا اثتمار هنالك ؛ فلذلك يجب به . وما ذكر فى الله فهو بقدرته ونفاذ مشيئته وجرى سلطانه ، فتمت ربوبيته واستوجب الجلال والرفعة بذاته ، لم يجز أن يكون فيه ما ذكر من الخوف ، بل به تمام الحكمة وعلو الرتبة ، وفى ايجاب ذلك لغيره نقض . ألا قول الثنوية بالذي ذكرت ، ولا يقبل قولهم بمعارضته مثله في الله أنه يقدر على أنه لا يفعل ، فإذا يقدر على نقض ربوبيته ، وبمثله جعلوه في غيره معارضاً موجبا ذلك ، فمثله الذي نحن فيه . وووجه آخر أنه لا يوجب لله قدرة ولا علما بقوله له قدرة بكذا وعلم بكذا لا معنى له ، وذلك متحقق في غيره ، ما علم
And the third [argument]: that the way of knowing the corruption of the statement of two [gods] is only the power of one of them over what the other does not know, and in that is the affirmation of that. And if this were permissible without there being in that a corruption of divinity, then the statement of the monotheists concerning it by which they refuted the statement of the dualists would be nullified. And His statement: that He knows, with the fact that he will not believe, how it would be – this is a meaning without benefit; because His knowledge of that is known – does he believe first? If he says: No, he insults him. And if he says: Yes, it is said: in that lies the demand. And I mentioned that if he believed, he would not exit from His knowledge, so how did he not exit? And His knowledge, and it was, and there is no power except with God. And as for his statement: 'If He had not empowered him, he would not be blameworthy,' it is like the statement equally; and its proof is his statement, rather upon him is greater blame; it is the product of power where he turned away from that by which he comes to it. And his statement: 'It does not necessitate us because power is not faith,' he also pushes back; for in it is what prevents the necessitation, rather it is necessitated only because power is not faith. Then his consideration by command is corrupt; because it is enslavement by it, manifesting his lowliness and servitude. And power is wealth, highness, and exaltation, so it is the aspect by which his lordship is invalidated from other than God, and that is not in the command. On the contrary, if there were no command nor prohibition, the statement of believing and disbelieving and being able and not being able would have no meaning, and there is no power except with God. And afterwards, power's fruit is action, and by it occurs what is mentioned, not by command; therefore issuing a command by a command is a command for what I mentioned, so by empowerment he becomes a rich king, a representative, so that when it is completed, he was a lord, a god, he was a lord, a god, and there is no power except with God. And afterwards, we countered with that whose path of knowledge is the intellect from the aspect I mentioned, and the command does not contradict what the intellect necessitates. And if there were no command, the first would be wild by intellect. As for knowing the aspect of wisdom in the command or not knowing it by what we know, it is not necessary to reject it by what is inaccessible to him of the second aspect, and there is no power except with God. And what I mentioned is supported by the matter of experience: that every powerful one is elevated and exalted by his power, and the majestic great is not commanded with anything; it is established that in command there is lowliness and servitude, so it does not necessitate, and in empowerment there is his elevation and highness, so it necessitates, and God is the granter of success. You see that – not that he has nullified. And afterwards, since no power exists that is wasted, its existence necessitates the action that is intended, and the right of command is obligation, not the occurrence of the action. How many a command is issued without compliance there? Therefore it is necessitated by it. And what is mentioned concerning God is by His power, the execution of His will, and the flow of His authority; thus His lordship is perfected and He deserves majesty and highness by His essence. It is not permissible that there be in Him what was mentioned of fear, rather by it is the completion of wisdom and highness of rank, and in necessitating that for other than Him is a contradiction. Indeed, the statement of the dualists is as I mentioned, and their statement is not accepted by countering it with the like in God: that He is able to not act, thus He is able to nullify His lordship. And by its like they made it in other than Him as a counter necessitating that, so its like is what we are in. And another aspect: that it does not necessitate for God power nor knowledge by his statement 'He has power over such-and-such and knowledge of such-and-such' has no meaning, and that is verified in other than Him, what is known.
Source pages p. 874

Commentary
This passage is a composite argument refuting dualism (the belief in two gods) and addressing related objections about divine command, power, and blame. Maturidi begins with the third argument: the only way to know the falsity of dualism is that each deity would have power over what the other does not know, implying mutual ignorance – a corruption of divinity. If this were acceptable, the monotheists' refutation would be null. He then addresses a statement: 'He knows that he will not believe – what benefit is there?' Maturidi argues it has no benefit because the knowledge is prior; questioning whether the person will believe or not leads to absurdities. Next, he refutes the objection 'If He had not empowered him, he would not be blameworthy' – on the contrary, blame is greater because it is the product of power turning away from what leads to faith. He clarifies that power is not faith, so blame is necessitated precisely because power is separate. Then he critiques the notion that command implies deficiency: command shows lowliness and servitude, while power shows exaltation. Empowerment makes one a rich king, while command without empowerment has no meaning. He argues that intellect shows the harmony between command and reason; without command, intellect would be wild. Finally, he affirms that for God, fear does not apply; His perfection is by His essence. He concludes by rejecting the dualists' counterargument that God has power not to act, which would imply nullification of His lordship – this is not comparable.

Key Terms
ثنوية
قدرة
علم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, how is the falsehood of dualism known?
A) By the fact that two gods cannot create jointly
B) By the fact that one god would have power over what the other does not know
C) By the fact that two gods would fight for control
D) By divine revelation

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of mutual ignorance in any deity proves the falsehood of dualism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have understood that ignorance corrupts divinity. But you are human and naturally ignorant. In what specific area of your life today are you acting as if you know it all, and how will you consciously surrender that to God's complete knowledge?
?
The dualist thought that two gods could coexist without interference mirrors our tendency to compartmentalize life—keeping parts away from God's authority. Where have you created a private zone you think God doesn't see or control? What will you do to acknowledge His all-encompassing knowledge over that zone right now?
?
If a single flaw—ignorance—makes a supposed deity unworthy of worship, how seriously do you take the small blind spots in your own life? Will you commit today to inviting God's light into your darkest ignorance, trusting that He alone knows what you do not?

Refuting Objections on Knowledge and Blame
Maturidi addresses objections about God's knowledge of disbelief and the link between empowerment and blame.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's responses to two objections: the alleged uselessness of God's knowledge of disbelief and the claim that lack of empowerment removes blame.

Core Idea
Blame is greater precisely because God gave power, and His knowledge of disbelief is not useless.

Dualists and others raise objections: if God knows a person will not believe, what benefit is that knowledge? And if He had not empowered the person, then he would not be blameworthy for disbelief. Maturidi refutes both with logical reasoning showing that blame is increased by the gift of power and that God's knowledge is prior and unchanging.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the knowledge objection
Read the first part about 'He knows that he will not believe' and Maturidi's response. Note the absurdity of questioning God's knowledge.
The language is terse; focus on the logic.
2
Grasp the blame objection and response
Read the second part about 'If He had not empowered him, he would not be blameworthy.' Notice that Maturidi says blame is even greater because power was misused.
He also clarifies that power is not the same as faith.
3
Connect the two arguments
Both objections stem from misunderstanding God's attributes. Maturidi affirms that God's knowledge is perfect and that human accountability is based on the power granted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And as for his statement: 'If He had not empowered him, he would not be blameworthy,' it is like the statement equally; and its proof is his statement, rather upon him is greater blame; it is the product of power where he turned away from that by which he comes to it. And his statement: 'It does not necessitate us because power is not faith,' he also pushes back; for in it is what prevents the necessitation, rather it is necessitated only because power is not faith. Then his consideration by command is corrupt; because it is enslavement by it, manifesting his lowliness and servitude. And power is wealth, highness, and exaltation, so it is the aspect by which his lordship is invalidated from other than God, and that is not in the command. On the contrary, if there were no command nor prohibition, the statement of believing and disbelieving and being able and not being able would have no meaning, and there is no power except with God. And afterwards, power's fruit is action, and by it occurs what is mentioned, not by command; therefore issuing a command by a command is a command for what I mentioned, so by empowerment he becomes a rich king, a representative, so that when it is completed, he was a lord, a god, he was a lord, a god, and there is no power except with God. And afterwards, we countered with that whose path of knowledge is the intellect from the aspect I mentioned, and the command does not contradict what the intellect necessitates. And if there were no command, the first would be wild by intellect. As for knowing the aspect of wisdom in the command or not knowing it by what we know, it is not necessary to reject it by what is inaccessible to him of the second aspect, and there is no power except with God. And what I mentioned is supported by the matter of experience: that every powerful one is elevated and exalted by his power, and the majestic great is not commanded with anything; it is established that in command there is lowliness and servitude, so it does not necessitate, and in empowerment there is his elevation and highness, so it necessitates, and God is the granter of success. You see that – not that he has nullified. And afterwards, since no power exists that is wasted, its existence necessitates the action that is intended, and the right of command is obligation, not the occurrence of the action. How many a command is issued without compliance there? Therefore it is necessitated by it. And what is mentioned concerning God is by His power, the execution of His will, and the flow of His authority; thus His lordship is perfected and He deserves majesty and highness by His essence. It is not permissible that there be in Him what was mentioned of fear, rather by it is the completion of wisdom and highness of rank, and in necessitating that for other than Him is a contradiction. Indeed, the statement of the dualists is as I mentioned, and their statement is not accepted by countering it with the like in God: that He is able to not act, thus He is able to nullify His lordship. And by its like they made it in other than Him as a counter necessitating that, so its like is what we are in. And another aspect: that it does not necessitate for God power nor knowledge by his statement 'He has power over such-and-such and knowledge of such-and-such' has no meaning, and that is verified in other than Him, what is known.
Source pages p. 874

Commentary
This passage is a composite argument refuting dualism (the belief in two gods) and addressing related objections about divine command, power, and blame. Maturidi begins with the third argument: the only way to know the falsity of dualism is that each deity would have power over what the other does not know, implying mutual ignorance – a corruption of divinity. If this were acceptable, the monotheists' refutation would be null. He then addresses a statement: 'He knows that he will not believe – what benefit is there?' Maturidi argues it has no benefit because the knowledge is prior; questioning whether the person will believe or not leads to absurdities. Next, he refutes the objection 'If He had not empowered him, he would not be blameworthy' – on the contrary, blame is greater because it is the product of power turning away from what leads to faith. He clarifies that power is not faith, so blame is necessitated precisely because power is separate. Then he critiques the notion that command implies deficiency: command shows lowliness and servitude, while power shows exaltation. Empowerment makes one a rich king, while command without empowerment has no meaning. He argues that intellect shows the harmony between command and reason; without command, intellect would be wild. Finally, he affirms that for God, fear does not apply; His perfection is by His essence. He concludes by rejecting the dualists' counterargument that God has power not to act, which would imply nullification of His lordship – this is not comparable.

Key Terms
علم
لائمة
قدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God did not give power, the person would not be blameworthy.
Correction: Maturidi says blame is even greater because the person misused God's power, and power is distinct from faith.

Checkpoint
How does Maturidi refute the objection that if God had not empowered a person, he would not be blameworthy?
A) By denying that God gives power
B) By stating that blame is even greater because the person misused God's power
C) By claiming that power and faith are the same
D) By accepting the objection

Key Takeaway
Blame is not removed by lack of empowerment; rather, empowerment increases accountability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you ever feel that God's knowledge of your sin beforehand makes your sin somehow less your fault? How does Maturidi's argument that knowledge does not necessitate excuse you from accountability?
?
Think of a time you used your God-given abilities to turn away from obedience. Does knowing that your empowerment increases your blame move you to repent? What will you change today?
?
The objection says: 'If God had not empowered me, I wouldn't be blamed.' Do you ever use that excuse? Maturidi says blame is greater because of empowerment. How does that challenge your self-justification?

Command vs Empowerment and Final Refutation
Maturidi argues that command is enslavement while empowerment is exaltation, and refutes dualist counterarguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that command implies lowliness while empowerment implies exaltation, and his final refutation of dualism against their counterarguments.

Core Idea
Command shows servitude; empowerment shows lordship; thus God's command does not imply deficiency.

Maturidi critiques the notion that command is degrading and argues that empowerment is what elevates. He then concludes with a final refutation of dualism, showing that their counterarguments do not apply to God. This passage ties together the themes of power, command, and lordship.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on command vs empowerment
Read from 'Then his consideration by command is corrupt' to 'and there is no power except with God'. Note how Maturidi contrasts command (degrading) with empowerment (exalting).
2
Understand the role of intellect
Maturidi argues that command does not contradict reason, and without command, intellect would be wild. This shows harmony between revelation and reason.
3
Follow the final refutation of dualism
Read the concluding part where Maturidi rejects the dualists' counterargument that God's ability not to act would nullify His lordship. He shows the comparison is invalid.
The text ends abruptly; focus on the argument.

Commentary

The Principle of Proportional Punishment
Maturidi introduces the first aspect: disparity of sins and proportional punishment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that God's punishment is based on wisdom and proportionality, and that eternal punishment is only for the gravest sins like shirk.

Core Idea
Justice demands that punishment be proportional to the sin.

Maturidi begins by establishing a foundational principle: God's actions are based on wisdom, not arbitrary choice. He argues that punishment must fit the crime, and since God has promised justice and is described with mercy, many sins are forgiven.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening statement
Read the Arabic passage that introduces the distinction between eternal and non-eternal punishment. Notice how Maturidi anchors the discussion in 'the way of wisdom' (min ṭarīq al-ḥikma).
The phrase 'من طريق الحكمة' sets the tone for a rational argument.
2
Identify the first aspect
Identify the first aspect: consideration of the disparity of sins themselves. Maturidi argues that God's wisdom requires that punishment be like for like, and that God is described with pardon and mercy, so forgiveness is ordained for many sins.
This connects to the principle of justice (ʿadl).
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this principle means that not all sins lead to eternal punishment. Ask yourself: How does God's attribute of mercy interact with His justice?
This lesson lays the groundwork for the next lesson on believers' sins.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم تذكر الفصل بين ما يخلد له العذاب ولا يخلد من طريق الحكمة وذلك يخرج على وجهين: أحدهما من طريق الاعتبار بتفاوت الذنوب في أنفسها وقد وعد الله أن لا يجزى إلا مثله، وكذلك حق الحكمة؛ إذ التعذيب يكون بما يوجبه الحكمة لا بما يختار؛ إذ ليس هو نوع ما يختار وبخاصة ممن لا يضره الخلاف، ثم هو الموصوف بالعفو والرحمة، ولهذا ما أوجب المغفرة والعفو عن كثير من الذنوب. ثم يخرج ذا على وجوه: أحدها أنه ما أحد يعصى الله بنوع من الكبائر دون الشرك إلا وهو لوقت العصيان مكتسب الطاعة من خوف عقاب، والنزع عن مقته، ورجاء رحمته، والثقة بكرمه، وذلك عن خيرات لو قوبل بها ما ارتكب من الخلاف بغلبة شهوة وقهر غضب أو نحو ذلك ليرجح ما كان له منه من خير على ما كان من شر، فلا يجوز، فلا يجوز أن يحرم نفع الخير ويوجب عقوبة الشر، ومن ذلك فعله موصوف بالجود والكرم ولا كذلك معناهما، ولا قوة إلا بالله. وليس مع من يكفر بالله ويشرك به معنى يستحق اسم الحسنة والخير؛ لأنه يكذبه وينكر أمره ونهيه، فلا يحتمل أن يكون له الرجا، وفى دوام عذابه مضادة معنى الكرم والجود، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Then he mentioned the distinction between that for which torment is eternal and that for which it is not eternal, from the way of wisdom. That is presented in two aspects: One of them is by way of consideration of the disparity of sins themselves, and Allah has promised that He will not recompense except with its like, and likewise the right of wisdom, for punishment is by what wisdom necessitates, not by what one chooses, for it is not a type of choice, especially from one whom opposition does not harm. Moreover, He is described with pardon and mercy, and for this reason He has ordained forgiveness and pardon for many sins. Then this is presented in aspects: One of them is that no one disobeys Allah with a type of major sin except shirk except that at the time of disobedience he is acquiring obedience from fear of punishment, withdrawal from His abhorrence, hope for His mercy, and trust in His generosity. And that is due to good deeds which, if compared to what he committed of opposition due to overwhelming desire and overpowering anger or the like, would cause his good to outweigh his evil. So it is not permissible, it is not permissible to deprive him of the benefit of good and impose the punishment of evil. And from that, His action is described with generosity and nobility, and not so their meanings. And there is no power except with Allah. And there is no meaning with him who disbelieves in Allah and associates partners with Him that deserves the name 'good deed' or 'goodness', because he denies Him and rejects His command and prohibition, so it is not possible for him to have hope, and in the permanence of his punishment there is contradiction to the meaning of generosity and nobility. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 959

Commentary
This passage addresses the distinction between eternal punishment (for disbelievers) and temporary punishment (for believing sinners). Maturidi gives two aspects: first, considering the disparity of sins themselves - God's justice requires proportional punishment. Second, he argues that any believer who commits major sins (other than shirk) still has some good deeds and obedience at the time of sin (fear of God, hope, etc.). These good deeds outweigh the sin, so it is not just to make punishment eternal. In contrast, the disbeliever has no good deeds at all, so eternal punishment is consistent with wisdom. This argument uses the principle that God's actions are based on wisdom and justice.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That all sins, including minor ones, lead to eternal punishment in Hell.
Correction: Maturidi argues that God's wisdom requires proportional punishment, and because He is merciful, many sins are forgiven. Only the gravest sins (like shirk) warrant eternal punishment.

B) Because He is described with pardon and mercy

Key Takeaway

?

?

Why Believers' Sins Are Not Eternal
Maturidi explains why believers' major sins do not lead to eternal punishment and contrasts with disbelievers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that believers who commit major sins (except shirk) still have good deeds that outweigh their sins, so their punishment cannot be eternal.

Core Idea
A believer's good deeds outshine his sins.

Building on the principle of proportional punishment, Maturidi now presents the second aspect of the distinction. He argues that even when a believer commits a major sin (other than shirk), he still performs acts of obedience like fearing God and hoping for mercy. These good deeds outweigh the sin, so eternal punishment would be unjust. In contrast, the disbeliever has no good deeds at all.

Guided Path
1
Read the second aspect
Read the Arabic passage beginning with 'ثم يخرج ذا على وجوه'. Notice that Maturidi gives a detailed reason for why the believer's punishment is not eternal.
The phrase 'أحدها' marks the first sub-aspect within the second main point.
2
Identify the believer's good deeds
List the good deeds Maturidi mentions: fear of punishment (khawf ʿiqāb), withdrawal from God's abhorrence (nazʿ ʿan maqti-hi), hope for mercy (rajāʾ raḥmati-hi), trust in generosity (thiqa bi-karmi-hi). These are present even during sin.
These good deeds are considered 'khayrāt' (good things) that outweigh the sin.
3
Contrast with the disbeliever
Read the final part where Maturidi states that the disbeliever has no meaning deserving the name 'good deed' or 'goodness', so eternal punishment is consistent with wisdom.
This contrast drives home the distinction.

ثم تذكر الفصل بين ما يخلد له العذاب ولا يخلد من طريق الحكمة وذلك يخرج على وجهين: أحدهما من طريق الاعتبار بتفاوت الذنوب في أنفسها وقد وعد الله أن لا يجزى إلا مثله، وكذلك حق الحكمة؛ إذ التعذيب يكون بما يوجبه الحكمة لا بما يختار؛ إذ ليس هو نوع ما يختار وبخاصة ممن لا يضره الخلاف، ثم هو الموصوف بالعفو والرحمة، ولهذا ما أوجب المغفرة والعفو عن كثير من الذنوب. ثم يخرج ذا على وجوه: أحدها أنه ما أحد يعصى الله بنوع من الكبائر دون الشرك إلا وهو لوقت العصيان مكتسب الطاعة من خوف عقاب، والنزع عن مقته، ورجاء رحمته، والثقة بكرمه، وذلك عن خيرات لو قوبل بها ما ارتكب من الخلاف بغلبة شهوة وقهر غضب أو نحو ذلك ليرجح ما كان له منه من خير على ما كان من شر، فلا يجوز، فلا يجوز أن يحرم نفع الخير ويوجب عقوبة الشر، ومن ذلك فعله موصوف بالجود والكرم ولا كذلك معناهما، ولا قوة إلا بالله. وليس مع من يكفر بالله ويشرك به معنى يستحق اسم الحسنة والخير؛ لأنه يكذبه وينكر أمره ونهيه، فلا يحتمل أن يكون له الرجا، وفى دوام عذابه مضادة معنى الكرم والجود، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Then he mentioned the distinction between that for which torment is eternal and that for which it is not eternal, from the way of wisdom. That is presented in two aspects: One of them is by way of consideration of the disparity of sins themselves, and Allah has promised that He will not recompense except with its like, and likewise the right of wisdom, for punishment is by what wisdom necessitates, not by what one chooses, for it is not a type of choice, especially from one whom opposition does not harm. Moreover, He is described with pardon and mercy, and for this reason He has ordained forgiveness and pardon for many sins. Then this is presented in aspects: One of them is that no one disobeys Allah with a type of major sin except shirk except that at the time of disobedience he is acquiring obedience from fear of punishment, withdrawal from His abhorrence, hope for His mercy, and trust in His generosity. And that is due to good deeds which, if compared to what he committed of opposition due to overwhelming desire and overpowering anger or the like, would cause his good to outweigh his evil. So it is not permissible, it is not permissible to deprive him of the benefit of good and impose the punishment of evil. And from that, His action is described with generosity and nobility, and not so their meanings. And there is no power except with Allah. And there is no meaning with him who disbelieves in Allah and associates partners with Him that deserves the name 'good deed' or 'goodness', because he denies Him and rejects His command and prohibition, so it is not possible for him to have hope, and in the permanence of his punishment there is contradiction to the meaning of generosity and nobility. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 959

Commentary
This passage addresses the distinction between eternal punishment (for disbelievers) and temporary punishment (for believing sinners). Maturidi gives two aspects: first, considering the disparity of sins themselves - God's justice requires proportional punishment. Second, he argues that any believer who commits major sins (other than shirk) still has some good deeds and obedience at the time of sin (fear of God, hope, etc.). These good deeds outweigh the sin, so it is not just to make punishment eternal. In contrast, the disbeliever has no good deeds at all, so eternal punishment is consistent with wisdom. This argument uses the principle that God's actions are based on wisdom and justice.

Key Terms
كبائر
شرك
خيرات
خوف
رجا
جود
كرم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That any sin, even by a believer, merits eternal punishment.
Correction: Maturidi argues that a believer's residual good deeds tip the scale, so eternal punishment would be unjust. Only disbelief (shirk) lacks any good deeds.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why does the believing sinner not deserve eternal punishment?
A) Because his sin is small
B) Because his good deeds outweigh his sin
C) Because God is unjust
D) Because he has no good deeds

Key Takeaway
A believer's eternal punishment is prevented by the good deeds he performs even in the act of sinning, while a disbeliever lacks such deeds.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a major sin you have committed. In that moment, did you have any fear of Allah's punishment or hope for His mercy? Or was your heart completely heedless? According to this lesson, those inner states are good deeds that could outweigh your sin. If you died in that state, would the balance of your deeds be in your favor? What does that reveal about the state of your heart when you sin?
?
This lesson says that a disbeliever has no good deeds because he denies Allah's commands. Are there any areas of your life where you act as if Allah's commands do not apply to you—where you deny His authority through your actions? How can you ensure that even in your moments of weakness, you maintain the fear and hope that define a believer?
?
Maturidi argues that eternal punishment for a believer with some good deeds would contradict Allah's generosity. Do you truly believe that Allah is generous enough to accept your meager good deeds and forgive your sins? Or do you despair of His mercy? What is your heart's actual trust in His generosity when you sin?

Allah's Existence
intermediate
6 min
Proof that Iman is Known (Ma'lum) Not Unknown
Maturidi argues that iman must be known because commands and revelation would otherwise be meaningless.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that iman must be known because divine commands and revelation would be meaningless if it were unknown.

Core Idea
Iman is a known reality, not an unknown abstraction.

Imam Maturidi addresses a key debate about the nature of iman: is it something known or unknown? He argues that if iman were unknown, then all divine commands and the sending of messengers would be futile. In this passage, he establishes that iman is a known reality, grounded in evidence from creation and revelation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the logical flow of Maturidi's argument.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the central dilemma
Find where Maturidi poses the two possibilities: iman is either known or unknown. Understand why this is the starting point.
This sets up the entire argument.
3
Trace the rejection of 'unknown'
See how Maturidi argues that if iman were unknown, then any claim about it (e.g., that it is not created) would be meaningless. Note the role of evidence.
He uses the lack of evidence for unknown things.
4
Identify the positive proof for 'known'
Look for the evidence Maturidi presents: sensory evidence that all besides Allah is created, and the revealed books as clear evidence. Conclude how this establishes iman as known.
The conclusion 'فثبت أنه معلوم' is the key.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم القول فى خلق الايمان فما بيننا وبين فريق من الحشوية ، ما قد بينا القول فى خلق أفعال العباد ما يكفى ذلك من تأمل أمر الإيمان ، أن الإيمان أن يكون معروفا أو مجهولا ، فإن كان مجهولا لا يعلمه أحد ، فنقول : من يقول بنفى الخلق لا معنى له ؛ لأن الذى يجهل حتى لا يصل إلى العلم به من طريق الدليل هو الخلق الذى لم يجعل الله فيما يشهده عليه دليلا يعرف مائيته وحقيقته ، وذلك خلق في جملة القول ، وبدلالة المحسوس على أن كل شيء سوى الله خلق ، كائن بعد أن لم يكن ، فأما الله تعالى وما يوصف به ففى الشاهد الله في جميع كتبه المنزلة دليل على التحقيق والإثبات ، فلا وجه للجهل به ، وفى ذلك تثبيت جعله ما لا يجوز الجهل به ؛ إذ الأمر بفعله عن [؟] ورسله الذين أرسلهم ، و به خوطب العباد بجميع شرائع الإسلام ، في حال يعرفها ؛ على الجهل بحقيقة ما به وجب التكليف وجرت به المحنة ، وعلى ذلك جرت البشارات ، وبالإغفال عنه جاء الإنذار والوعيد ، وعلى ذلك اتفق قول الأمة على علم إذ اختلافهم في الإضافة إلى ما يعقله الخلق ، فثبت أنه معلوم .
Then the statement regarding the creation of iman. What is between us and a group of the Hashwiyya is that we have already stated the statement on the creation of the acts of servants, and that suffices for considering the matter of iman: that iman is either known or unknown. If it is unknown and no one knows it, then we say: the one who asserts the negation of creation has no meaning; because that which is unknown to the point that one cannot arrive at knowledge of it by way of evidence is the creation that Allah has not placed in what we witness an evidence by which its essence and reality can be known. That is creation in general terms. And by the evidence of the senses that everything other than Allah is created, coming into existence after not having existed. As for Allah Most High and what is described by Him, in the witnessed world there is in all His revealed books an evidence for verification and affirmation, so there is no room for ignorance of Him. In that is confirmation that He made it something not permissible to be ignorant of. For the command to act upon it came from [؟] and His messengers whom He sent, and with it the servants were addressed with all the laws of Islam, in a state they know; upon ignorance of the reality of that which made obligation necessary and by which the trial occurred, and upon that came the glad tidings, and by negligence of it came the warnings and threats. Upon that the statement of the community agreed concerning knowledge since their difference is regarding the attribution to what the creation understands, so it is established that it is known.
Source pages p. 984

Commentary
This passage is a proof. Maturidi begins by referencing earlier discussion on creation of acts to set context. He poses a dilemma: iman is either known (ma'lum) or unknown (majhul). If it were unknown, then claiming it is not created is meaningless because what is truly unknown cannot be discussed. He argues that everything unknown without evidence is essentially a creature not manifested by God. But iman is known through sensory evidence that all besides God is created, and through revelation—God's books provide clear evidence. Therefore ignorance of iman is impossible. Commands and the sending of messengers further confirm iman is known. He concludes that the Ummah agrees iman is known, despite differences in attribution. Thus iman is established as known.

Key Terms
خلق الإيمان
معلوم
مجهول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think iman is an abstract unknown that cannot be understood or defined.
Correction: Maturidi argues that iman is a known reality, supported by sensory evidence and revelation. Commands and warnings from Allah would be meaningless if iman were unknown.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of Maturidi's argument in this passage?
A) Iman is unknown
B) Iman is known because of commands and revelation
C) Iman is created
D) Iman is eternal

Key Takeaway
Iman is a known reality established by evidence from creation and revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have clear evidence from creation and revelation that iman is known, yet you sometimes act as if doubt is an option. What are you afraid to admit by ignoring this certainty?
?
If iman is known and you are accountable for it, how will you answer to Allah for the moments you lived as if His commands were unclear?
?
The glad tidings and warnings are built on the reality that iman is known. Knowing this, what step will you take today to act on what you know to be true, not what feels convenient?
